==PAGE:179==
Chapter 9
Conclusion
This chapter contains the пђБnal conclusion of the work related to this masterвАЩs thesis. Suggestions
for further work will be given at the end of this chapter.
9.1
Summary and Conclusion
The objective of this masterвАЩs thesis has been related to natural soft clay and the characteristic
features which dominate its mechanical behaviour.
The mechanical behaviour of natural soft clay has been studied through a literature study to
identify the characteristic features which have been observed in the пђБeld and in the laboratory.
These features are mainly associated to viscous and ageing eпђАects and may dominate the mechanical
response with respect to natural soft clay. The main characteristics have been outlined as:
¬И Structure
¬И Creep
¬И Rate-dependency
Where the term, structure, consist of the fabric representing the arrangement of the particles and
pore space, and the bonding representing the inter-particle forces between the clay particles and
the water. These characteristics have created the framework for a new constitutive model known
as the SCA-R which stands for: Structure Creep Anisotropy вАУ Recovering. The constitutive model
is based on eпђАective stresses, utilizes the ModiпђБed Cam Clay (MCC) model and invariants as a
framework. The MCC response may be obtained by setting the additional input parameters, re-
lated to the natural soft clay response, to zero.
The anisotropy is introduced as an initial rotated and skewed yield surface.
The evolution in
anisotropy corresponds to rotation of the inclined yield surface. The elastic anisotropy is imple-
mented through an elastic anisotropic parameter which increase or decrease the stiпђАness in vertical
or horizontal direction similar to a modiпђБed version of Houlsby and Graham (1983) anisotropy
factor. The creep and rate-dependency are simulated through a time evolution of the plastic mul-
tiplier suggested by Grimstad (2009). The evolution is based on a time resistance concept suggested
by Janbu (1969) which utilizes one-dimensional evolution in the general three-dimensional space.
The bonding between the clay particles is implemented through an intrinsic yield surface and an
associated surface represented by the intrinsic properties and the amount of bonding. The model
introduces a method to regain the lost structure (bonding) over time. This feature yields an ad-
ditional parameter to control the visco-plastic strains through a induced coupling term. However,
more testing is required to ensure its capability to capture real soil response.
151
==PAGE:180==
9.2.
Suggestions for Further work
152
The model requires 17 input parameters to access all of its implemented features. Three additional
input parameters are available to switch between diпђАerent methods of simulating the stress history,
evolution of anisotropy, and failure criteria in the general stress space. The model assumes associ-
ated пђВow and thereby introduces eight state (hardening) parameters in total. The SCA-R model
is implemented as a user deпђБned soil model in PLAXIS through the interface between PLAXIS
and FORTRAN. It utilizes a simple forward Euler scheme to solve the local integration required
by PLAXIS.
The necessary soil parameters associated to the SCA-R model have been interpreted from several
laboratory tests with respect to the relevant stress domain. These parameters have been calibrated
by performing several back-calculations of the CRS and undrained tri-axial tests which have been
used to interpret the initial input parameters. These back-calculations have shown that the SCA-R
model is capable of capturing the main characteristics which have been observed in natural soft
clay and only minor modiпђБcations of the interpreted laboratory parameters were necessary.
As a пђБnal test, a full scale boundary value problem for the embankment located at Ons√Єy in Norway
has been performed. The SCA-R model has shown capability to capture the soil response and the
dissipation of excess pore water as expected and yields fairly acceptable results compared to пђБeld
measurements. The predictions of the settlement, excess pore pressure and horizontal displace-
ment have been compared to predictions from пђБve other soil models. The main conclusion states
that the SCA-R model yields quite accurate results according to the predictions of settlement and
excess pore pressure. However, because of its limitation to small stiпђАness, the SCA-R model yield
less accurate results compared to soil models which have incorporated this feature. However, the
prediction is still relatively fair compared to the пђБeld measurements.
The performance of the forward Euler scheme has been tested against an implicit scheme utilizing
a Newton-Raphson iteration to solve the diпђАerential equations in the local integration. During the
performance investigation complications arose and the predictions of the two integration schemes
were not similar. However, it is believed that this is a consequence of an error when combining
the code from the explicit scheme to the implicit scheme. This conclusion is supported by unre-
alistic predictions of the structure in the implicit scheme. The step size associated to the explicit
scheme has been investigated. Changing the restrictions, which control the maximum time and
strain increment, by one order of magnitude have showed no sensitivity in the пђБnal predictions of
the embankment. It is believed that PLAXIS applied a time and strain increment, for the given
case, which yields an accurate result without the restrictions. However, more veriпђБcation is needed
because of some unexplained observations when comparing the explicit and implicit schemes.
The recovery of structure has not been activated in the пђБnal prediction of the embankment. How-
ever, the eпђАect of activating the recovery on the пђБnal prediction has been investigated. It may be
concluded that lesser visco-plastic strains were generated and a higher amount of structure were
left at the long term, as expected. In addition, the recovery of structure has been investigated on a
reconstituted sample. The implemented feature is able to simulate a similar response as observed
by laboratory tests. However, this has not been compared to any speciпђБc laboratory test and fur-
ther research and testing are required before the recovery eпђАect may be dismissed or acknowledged.
It may be concluded that a user friendly soil model which is able to capture the main character-
istic features in natural soft clay has been created. The models seems quite robust with similar
computational time as for other available soil models in PLAXIS. However, further veriпђБcation is
necessary before the model пђБnally can be acknowledged.
9.2
Suggestions for Further work
The implemented feature, related to the recovering of structure, should be investigated in greater
detail to verify whether the model is capable of capturing more of the behaviour observed in the
laboratory and in the пђБeld. For the short term, laboratory tests performed on reconstituted clays
Jesper Bjerre (s102905)
==PAGE:181==
9.2.
Suggestions for Further work
153
have shown that a waiting period of a few days change the mechanical behaviour during tri-axial
testing which is not associated to creep (Sorensen, 2006). Regarding the long term, during larger
infrastructure projects a huge amount of natural soft clay may be removed and seen as a waste
of material. However, this material could be utilized if the initial mechanical properties and its
evolution over time were known.
The SCA-R model may be further extended to capture the soil response in greater details:
¬И Predictions from the embankment located at Ons√Єy have shown that the lack of small stiпђАness
causes large strains in areas where the strains should have a minor magnitude. Hence, the
performance in the small strain domain (i.e. shear strains below 10вИТ5) should be optimized
to obtain more realistic predictions. In addition, the SCA-R assumes a constant PoissonвАЩs
ratio which may trigger a too large stiпђАness during cyclic loading.
¬И The assumption of associated пђВow could be changed so that the SCA-R model uses non-
associated пђВow as e.g. the n-SAC or the SANICLAY model. Hence, the model will contain a
separate yield and plastic potential surface which are controlled by diпђАerent inclinations and
evolution laws, see Figure 9.1(a). Hence, one may apply the rotational hardening suggested
by Wheeler et al. (2003), which is restricted by high friction angles, to the plastic potential
and utilize another approach for the yield surface.
¬И With respect to the recovery of structure for the long term, the model could be extended to
simulate ground improvement in form of cementing the soil. This has been done in the MCC
model by (Horpibulsuk and Liu, 2009) and the principle may be seen on Figure 9.1(b).
Other improvements or extensions of the SCA-R may be suggested. However, one should keep
in mind that the majority of these aspects require additional input parameters. Increasing the
number of input parameters would most likely not be beneпђБcial if the SCA-R should be utilized
outside the research society.

Another improvement, which does not requires any additional input parameters, is related to the
solution technique of the local integration. The forward Euler scheme could be changed to a more
advanced explicit scheme or the implicit approach suggested by Roenningen et al. (2014).
In
addition, it is suggested to develop a software which may be used to interpret and calibrate the
input parameters since it may be confusing for people who are inexperienced in natural soft clay
and how these features are modeled.

Appendix C
Mathematical Derivations
This appendix contains additional theory and derivations which have been left out of the main
report.
C.1
Continuum Mechanics
A short introduction to continuum mechanics will be given to minimise the confusion regarding the
utilized notation, references and deпђБnitions which may vary with respect to the пђБeld of knowledge
and the background of the reader. For instance, in geotechnical engineering compression will be
considered as positive compared to structural engineering where tension normally is considered as
positive.
Soil as a material may be regarded as a relatively complex material. For simplicity reasons, soil
is divided into two phases: solid particles and pores. However, soil is often considered as a three
phases material since the pores may contain water and/or gas (normally air).
The saturation
degree in the soil may vary and three states are commonly deпђБned as: dry, partly saturated or
fully saturated. These states are very important with respect to stiпђАness and strength of the soil
since these properties are associated with the eпђАective stresses (stiпђАness of the grain skeleton). The
concept of eпђАective stresses was introduced by Terzaghi in 1923 (Nordal, 2014c) and relates the
total stresses (ѕГ), pore pressure (u) and eпђАective stresses (ѕГвА≤) for fully saturated soils as:
ѕГij = ѕГвА≤
ij + ќіiju
where
ќіij =
(

if i = j
0
if i ћЄ= j.
i, j = 1, 2, 3
(C.1)
Notice, the water only inпђВuences the principle directions through KroneckerвАЩs delta (ќіij) since
water cannot withstand any shear forces. The indices in Eq. (C.1) refers to the three Cartesian
axes (x1, x2, x3).

C.1.7
Bulk and Shear modulus
Bulk Modulus: The reason for using the bulk modulus (K) in soil mechanicss, during isotropic
elasticity, can be seen by introducing the mean stress:
pвА≤ = 1
3 (ѕГвА≤
11 + ѕГвА≤
22 + ѕГвА≤
33)
(C.25)
Utilizing the constitutive matrix the stresses can be rewritten into stiпђАness and strains and by
some algebra it can be shown that:
pвА≤ = K (ќµ11 + ќµ22 + ќµ33) = Kќµp
(C.26)
Hence, it can be seen that the volumetric strain is proportional to the mean eпђАective stress through
the bulk modulus for isotropic elastic conditions.

C.2.
CC vs. MCC
14
C.2
CC vs. MCC
The diпђАerence between the original and the ModiпђБed Cam Clay (MCC) is the shape of the yield
surface. In the original formulation the yield surface was theoretically derived based on an energy
equation. The shape has a logarithmic spiral format compared to the ellipse shape in the MCC, see
Figure C.3. However, the inconvenient shape in the CC model leads to numerical issues at the tip
of the yield surface where unrealistic plastic shear strain increments during isotropic compression
were predicted (Hokkaido).
Figure C.3: DiпђАerence between CC and MCC. Notice notation diпђАerence, p0 = pвА≤
c.
The evolution of the dilatation is associated to a linear relationship between the volumetric strain
and the stress ratio in the original whereas the MCC uses a logarithmic behaviour, see Figure C.4
(Ohta, 2007).

C.2.1
Derivation of Yield Surface
The shape of the yield surface in the MCC model is deпђБned as an ellipse in the (p : q)-space
(Ellipsoid in the principle space). The yield surface is governed by the material parameter M
which deпђБnes the Critical State Line (CSL). The CSL is associated to the Critical State (CS). For
more details about the CS see Appendix C.3. As a characteristic feature, the top point of the
ellipse would always touch through the CSL, see Figure C.5.
Jesper Bjerre (s102905)

C.3.
Critical State
16
C.3
Critical State
The Critical State (CS) may be formulated as suggested by (Nordal, 2014a):
DeпђБnition 10. A Critical State (CS) is a state where large shear strains may be applied without
any change in eпђАective stresses or in volume.
The CS represents a state where the soil is completely remoulded and may be seen as homogeneous.
The state is emerged since the plastic potentials are assumed to have a slope in the (pвА≤ : q)-plane
of dq/dpвА≤ = 0, at the a particular stress ratio (Wood, 1990):
qcs = Mpcs
and
ќЈcs = qcs
pвА≤cs
(C.34)
To each speciпђБc volume (v), a critical state may be found by the eпђАective mean pressure at CS:
vcs = ќУ вИТќї ln pвА≤
cs
(C.35)
where:
ќУ = N вИТ(ќї вИТќЇ) ln(2)
(C.36)
The soil response is associated to the OCR when approaching the CSL. For large OCR the soil
will show dilate behaviour and the contractive behaviour for small OCR. Introducing the CSL it
may be drawn in a three-dimensional space along with the NCL, see Figure C.6.
Figure C.6: State boundary surface. Taken from (Hokkaido).
As illustrated in Figure C.6, the CSL and the yield surfaces generate a boundary surface, often
known as the State Boundary Surface (SBS). The inside of SBS represent the elastic domain and
on the surface of SBS the plastic domain is represented. Notice, outside of the SBS is impossible.
Furthermore, the plastic potential will be normal to the SBS. The SBS may be used as a graphical
tool to easily see the eпђАect of compacting regarding to the shear strength of a soil.
Jesper Bjerre (s102905)
==PAGE:213==
C.3.
Critical State
17
Drained vs. Undrained
A drained or undrained condition are two very diпђАerent scenarios which will dominate the soil
response. Hence, general understanding of how the constitutive model behave in these conditions
must be assembled.
Drained
In a conventional drained tri-axial test, the EпђАective Stress Part (ESP) and the Total Stress Path
(TSP), in the pвА≤ : q space, will be known since the cell pressure will be kept constant during the
test. According to the deпђБnition of drained condition, the ESP and TSP will be equal. It may be
derived that the inclination would be 1:3 in the (pвА≤ : q)-space. Thereby, the stress increment can
be formulated as:
1
3 = dpвА≤
dq
,
dѕГ =
 dq
3
dq

(C.37)
When the ESP approaches the yield surface the following two situations may occur:
¬И Approaching in the dry region: Plastic expansion will occur resulting in a shrinking
yield surface. The ESP will follow the same path back until it reaches the CSL.
¬И Approaching in the wet region: Plastic compression will occur resulting in a expansion
of the yield surface. The expansion will stop approaching the CSL.
The demanding criteria, if the ESP will approach the yield surface in the wet or dry region, is
determined by the OCR. These two scenarios may be seen in Figure C.7(a) and C.7(b).
Undrained
In a conventional undrained tri-axial test no dissipation of pore water will occur since the pore
water will be trapped inside the sample by closing the valve during shearing. Thereby, the volume
will be kept constant and no volumetric strain will occur:
dќµp = 0
(C.38)
Notice that it has been assumed that full saturation is achieved prior to shearing, and water and
soil particles are incompressible. According to Eq. (2.57) and Eq. (C.38) the incremental stress
will only be dependent on the incremental shear strain:
dќµ =
 0
dќµq

(C.39)
The ESP may be estimated utilizing the criterion of constant volume. In the elastic domain the
volumetric change will only be related to eпђАective mean stress. Hence, no volumetric change will
result in a vertical ESP until it reaches the yield surface. As in the drained case, the TSP will still
have an inclination on 1:3 and the diпђАerence between ESP and TSP corresponds to the developed
excess pore pressure. In the plastic domain a change in eпђАective mean pressure may occur since
a change does not result in a change in volume in contrast to the elastic domain. Recalling the
deпђБnition of change in strain caused by eпђАective mean pressure:
dќµp = dќµe

¬И Approaching in the dry region: Plastic expansion will occur resulting in a shrinking
yield surface (decrease in pвА≤

it reach the CSL.

C.5.
Time Resistance Concept

C.5
Time Resistance Concept
The time resistance concept was suggested by Janbu (1969) which is a method to determine the
inпђВuence of creep by a single creep parameter. The deпђБnition of resistance is normally deпђБned as:
Resistance = Cause
EпђАect
Transforming this principle into creep methodology, the resistance parameter may be deпђБned as:
R = dt
dќµ = Increment in time
Increment in strain
(C.49)
Recalling that creep is associated to deformation over time corresponding to eпђАect and cause,
respectively. The time resistance concept is a simpliпђБcation of the reality since the method as-
sumes one-dimensional compression. However, the majority of deformation caused by creep occurs
vertically during normal scenarios (ageing of young deposits). Through the assumption of one-
dimensional, the magnitude of creep may be determined by one single parameter determined from
a one-dimensional testing equipment. The principle of JanbuвАЩs stress-strain-time behaviour may
be seen on Figure C.13.
Figure C.13: JanbuвАЩs stress-strain-time concept. Taken from (Janbu, 1998).
As seen on Figure C.13, the time resistance number (rs) may be determined as the inclination
during the secondary compression.

and Taiebat, 2013).

Loading to critical state and uniqueness of CSL:
In the main essence of critical state theory it requires that the critical state ratio ќЈ = ќЈc = M
at the state where вИВQ
вИВp = 0 meaning that the rate of pвА≤

evolution law the same requirement should be fulпђБlled. To do this the bounding value of ќ± should
approach a critical value simultaneously with isotropic hardening and the combination of ќ±c and
ќЈc guarantees a unique critical state line in the (e : ln pвА≤)-space.
The пђБrst step in this analyses process is to rewrite the formulation for the yield surface to obtain
a relationship between the stress conditions and rotation parameter in the critical state:

conпђБrmed if the ratio ќ±c/M remains the same for any loading at any mean pressure pвА≤ and yielding
the same predictions of the critical state.
Going back to the four evolution laws the пђБrst would never yield a unique critical state line due to
the term of |вИВG/вИВp|. As stated in Dafalias and Taiebat (2013), this term would freeze the evolution
of both ќ± and pвА≤

by applying non-associated пђВow the simulation capability is improved dramatically.

Appendix D
Programming
The purpose of this appendix is to provide information regarding the constitutive model. The пђБrst
part will introduce the algorithm and the procedure related to the MCC model which has been
programmed in MATLAB. The second part will present the FORTRAN framework.
D.1
Algorithm for the MCC Model
The MCC model in MATLAB is an iterative procedure where stresses are determined by a known
vertical deformation. A pseudo code may be seen in Algorithm 1.
Data: ќЇ, ќї, ќљ, e0, M and pвА≤
c
Result: pвА≤
cs, qcs, u, ќµ11, ќµp and ќµd
Initialization by assembling the initial contributions in vectors and matrices
while Counter < Max number of iterations do
if F = 0 then
Determine new pвА≤
0 by the previous stresses
else
No change in pвА≤
c
end
if yield = 0 then
Compute dќї
else
dќї = 0
end
-Assemble the constitutive stiпђАness matrix
-Determine Incremental strains
-Determine Incremental stress
-Updating stresses, strains and state parameters
end
Algorithm 1: MCC in MATLAB
The MATLAB code can be found in Appendix E.1.1. In general, the code has been assembled
by the elastic and plastic theory given by Chapter 2. The assembling of the constitutive stiпђАness
matrix will be explained in the following section.

D.1.1
Determination of Strains
The strains may be divided into drained and undrained with respect to tri-axial conditions:
¬И In undrained conditions the strains in the horizontal direction would be equal (ќµ22 = ќµ33)
and obtain the half of the vertical strain since no volume change is allowed.
¬И In drained conditions the horizontal strain is determined by utilizing the fact that no change
in stresses will occur on a free surface.
Drained condition: For elastic conditions the change in stresses may be formulated as:
вИЖѕГвА≤
11 = вИЖќµ11D11 + вИЖќµ22D12 + вИЖќµ33D13
(D.7a)
вИЖѕГвА≤
22 = вИЖќµ11D21 + вИЖќµ22D22 + вИЖќµ33D23
(D.7b)
вИЖѕГвА≤
33 = вИЖќµ11D31 + вИЖќµ22D32 + вИЖќµ33D33
(D.7c)
Under triaxial conditions following relations can be assumed.
вИЖѕГ22 = вИЖѕГ33 = 0
and
вИЖќµ22 = вИЖќµ33
(D.8)
Thereby, expressions for the horizontal strains may be formulated as:
вИЖќµ22 = вИТвИЖќµ11D21
D22 + D23
and
вИЖќµ33 = вИТвИЖќµ11D31
D33 + D31
(D.9)
Notice that the elastic relationship is used. This approximation is only valid if very small steps
are utilized:

ipl
=
Plasticity indicator
0 = No plasticity

All the arguments above consists of standard type, i.e. parameters starting with the letters A-H
and O-Z are associated to double (8-byte) пђВoating points. The parameters may be divided into:
¬И Input: IDTask, iMod, IsUndr, iStep, iTer, Ier, Int, X, Y, Z, Time0, dTime, Props,
Sig0, Swp0, StVar0, dEps uPrjDir and iPrjLen.
¬И Output: D, Bulk W, Sig, Swp, StVar, ipl, nStat, NonSym, iStrsDep, iTimeDep, iTang
and iAbort.
The input parameters are provided by PLAXIS and should be within the subroutine where the
output parameters should be chosen by the user. Note, in the case of IDTask = 1 then StVar0
becomes an output parameter.
D.3.1
DLL File - Location
The created DLL пђБle must be placed in the correct directory to be able to be used in PLAXIS.
Depending on the version of PLAXIS the пђБle must be placed at diпђАerent locations in the installation
folder. As mentioned in Section 1.6 the PLAXIS version is PLAXIS 2D AE 2.0. The correct location
of this version is:
¬И C:\Program Files\Plaxis\Plaxis 2D\udsm\ Your DLL пђБle
Beware of other locations of the installation folder. Using other versions of PLAXIS please look at
the PLAXIS homepage at: http://kb.plaxis.nl/models/plaxis-user-defined-soil-models.
The bit version of the DLL пђБle may cause issues when trying to run the UDSM in PLAXIS.
The calculation kernel in PLAXIS uses 64-bit applications which are not able to communicate
with a 32-bit DLL пђБle. The problem cannot be solved by creating a 64-bit DLL пђБle since the
Graphical User Interface (GUI) is only available as a 32-bit application. Therefore, a 32-bit and
64-bit DLL пђБle must be created and placed in the folder mentioned above. This approach has
been used to run the UDSM in PLAXIS. Otherwise, it is possible to force PLAXIS to use the
32-bit kernel by removing or renaming the 64-bit kernel from the installation folder. For more
information about changing to a 32-bit kernel, please look at the PLAXIS homepage: http:
//kb.plaxis.nl/tips-and-tricks/64-bit-user-defined-soil-model.
Jesper Bjerre (s102905)

D.3.2
Finding the UDSM in PLAXIS
When the UDSM is created and the DLL пђБles (32-bit and 64-bit) are located at the correct location
the UDSM can be selected as a soil model in PLAXIS. This is done by choosing the User-deпђБned
option in the Material model combo box in the General tabsheet when deпђБning new soils, see
Figure D.5.
Figure D.5: Selecting the UDSM.
Next step is done in the Parameters tabsheet. At the combo box вАЭDLL пђБleвАЭ the created DLL пђБle
is selected, see Figure D.6. Note that only one DLL пђБle will be available even if both a 32-bit and
a 64 bit DLL пђБle are created.
Figure D.6: Choosing the correct DLL пђБle.
The DLL пђБle may contain more soil models. Therefore the wanted UDSM (if more are available)
needs to be chosen which is done in the Model in DLL combo box, see Figure D.7. Note that some
PLAXIS versions have a bug and do not show the combo box clearly. If the user just clicks where
it should be, a drop down window should appear.

D.4.
Macauley Brackets

Figure D.7: Selecting the wanted soil model in the DLL пђБle.
PLAXIS will automatically read the input parameters from the DLL пђБle and they should be
visible in the Parameters tabsheet just below the Model in DLL combo box. The rest of the soil
properties can be added to the soil model. Note that the Interfaces are slightly diпђАerent in a
UDSM. It contains the oedometers modulus (Eref
oed), the interface strength parameters cinter, ѕХinter
and ѕИinter. Hence the interface shear strength is directly given in strength parameters instead of
using a factor relating the interface shear strength to the soil shear strength (PLAXIS, 2015).
D.4
Macauley Brackets
The Macauley brackets is a mathematical tool which yields the same magnitude as input if the
input is positive and zero if the input is negative or zero.
MATLAB: The heaviside-function is used to describe the Macauley bracket. This is a built-in
function in MATLAB returning following output:
вЯ®xвЯ©= x ¬Ј heaviside(x)
where
heaviside(x) =
п£±
п£і
п£≤
п£і
п£≥
0
if x < 0
1
2
if x = 0
1
if x > 0
(D.10)
As observed in Eq. (D.10) one could expect errors when the input value is zero caused by the
heaviside-function yielding a value of 1
2. Hence, it deviates from the deпђБnition of the Macauley
brackets. However, the heaviside-function is multiplied by the input value which solve the issue.
FORTRAN: Converting to FORTRAN code, the built-in functions in MATLAB will not be
transferred. Therefore, in the generated code from MATLAB an unknown function call heaviside
is present. This function is created in FORTRAN by the following procedure:
if x > 0.0 then
y = 1.0
else if x == 0.0 then
y = 0.5
else
y = 0.0
end if

%============================ Input Parameters ===========================%
k = 0.05;
% Swelling index
l = 0.25;
% Compression index
nu = 0.15;
% Poissons ratio
M = 1;
% Stress ratio (critical state)
N = 3.4259;
% Location of isotrop0c normal
pcc = 300;
% Preconsolidation pressure
p0 = 300;
% Initial pressure
%========================= Initial Calculations ==========================%
%% Determine following initial parameters V,e0 and OCR

% Renaming

% Specific Volume

% Initial Void Ratio
OCR = pcc/p0;
% Over Consolidation Ratio

%=========================== Numerical setup =============================%
%% Number of iterations and vertical strain]
iteration = 10000;
% Number of iterations
% (should not be lower than 10000)
ide
=0.01;
% In decimal
de = ide/100;
% Determining the procentence

% Determine the vertical strains
n = 1;
% Iterator
%====================== Initial vectors and matrice ======================%
%% Setup of the dimension og necessary vectors and matrice.
D = zeros(6,6);
% Stiffness Matrix
p = zeros(iteration,1);
% Mean Effective Stress
q = zeros(iteration,1);
% Deviatoric Stress

% Pore Water Pressure

% Void ratio

% Volumetic Strain

% Deviatoric Strain

dfdep=zeros(6,1);
% The Plastic Strains
dfds=zeros(6,1);
% The Stress Tensor
%============================ Initialize =================================%
%% Stresses, Strains, Yield Surface and Void Ratio
Sig=p0*[1;1;1;0;0;0];
% Stress
p(n)=(Sig(1)+2*Sig(3))/3;
% Effective Mean Stress
q(n)=(Sig(1)вИТSig(3));
% Deviatoric Stress
yield=(q(n)ЋЖ2/MЋЖ2+p(n)ЋЖ2)вИТp(n)*pc;
% Defining the yield surface
strain=[0;0;0;0;0;0];
% Strain
void(n)=e0;
% Void Ratio
%% Plotting the initial yield surface
p ini yield=(0:pc);
% Effective Mean pressure
qy = (MЋЖ2*(pc*p ini yieldвИТ...
p ini yield.ЋЖ2)).ЋЖ0.5;
% Deviatoric Stress
%====================== While loop (Iteration process) ===================%
% While loop creates the elastic stiffness matrix and constitutive matrix.
% Checking if the yield condition i fulfilled and if yes computing the
% stresses with respect to the constitutive matrix. If the yield criteria
% is below zero when only the elastic part is taken. Furhtermore, updating
% all parameters which changes in each increment.
while n<iteration
K=v*p(n)/k;
% Bulk Modulus
G=(3*K*(1вИТ2*nu))/(2*(1+nu));
% Shear Modulus
if yield==0, pc=(q(n)ЋЖ2/MЋЖ2+p(n)ЋЖ2)/p(n);
% New preconsolidation
else pc=pcc;
% No yielding вИТвИТ> no change
end
%% Determine the Elastic Stiffness, plastic stiffness and subcalculations.
for i=1:6
% The stiffness matrix 6x6
for j=1:6
% The stiffness matrix 6x6
if i<=3
if yield <0, dfds(i,1)=0;dfdep(i,1)=0; % No platicity!
else
% Platicity ! вИТвИТ>
dfds(i,1)=(2*p(n)вИТpc)/3 + 3*(Sig(i)вИТp(n))/MЋЖ2;
% dfds
dfdep(i,1)=(вИТp(n))*pc*(1+e0)/(lвИТk)*MЋЖ2;
% dfdep
end
if i==j
% First 3 in the diagonal
D(i,j)= K+4/3*G;
% Elastic Stiffness 1/3
else if i<=3, D(i,j)=KвИТ2/3*G;
% Elastic stiffness 2/3 % obs
end
end
end
if i>3, dfds(i,1)=0; dfdep(i,1)=0;
% df/ds',df/dep (triвИТAxial)
if i==j, D(i,j)= G;
% Elastic Stiffness 3/3
else
D(i,j)=0;
% Setting rest to zero
end
end
Jesper Bjerre (s102905)
==PAGE:241==
E.1.
MATLAB
45
end
end
%% Constrution the Stiffness Matrix, either elastic or platic
if yield<0, Dep=D;
% Elastic
else Dep=DвИТ((D*dfds*(dfds')*D)/...
((вИТ(dfdep')*dfds+(dfds')*D*dfds)));
% Plastic
end
%============================== Updating =================================%
%% Incremental strains, Note first line Undrained, second drained!
%
dStrain = [de;вИТde/2.;вИТde/2.;0.;0.;0.];
% (TriвИТAxial)
dStrain = [de;вИТ1*Dep(2,1)/(Dep(2,2)+Dep(2,3))*de;вИТ1*Dep(3,1)/...
(Dep(3,2)+Dep(3,3))*de;0.;0.;0.];
%% Incremental stress
dS = Dep*dStrain;
%% New strain and stresses
Sig = Sig+dS;
strain = strain+dStrain;
%% Incremental volumetric and deviatoric strains
deps p = dStrain(1) + dStrain (2) + dStrain (3); % Volumetric
deps d = 2./3. * (dStrain(1) вИТdStrain(3));
% Deviatoric
%% Update Specific Volume
v = NвИТ((k*log(p(n)/pc))+l*log(pc));
%% Updating Iteration, Effective Mean Stress, Shear stress, Pore Pressure
n = n+1;
p(n) = (Sig(1)+Sig(2)+Sig(3))/3;
q(n) = Sig(1)вИТSig(3);
u(n) = p0+q(n)/3вИТp(n);
%% Updating Void Ratio, Final Strain Volume, Deviator Strain
void(n) = vвИТ1.0;
eps p(n) = deps p + eps p(nвИТ1);
eps d(n) = deps d + eps d(nвИТ1);
%% Defining the yield surface at the partical increment
if yield<0, yield=q(n)ЋЖ2+MЋЖ2*p(n)ЋЖ2вИТMЋЖ2*p(n)*pc;
else yield=0;
end
end
%============== Determining stuff for plotting ===========================%
%% Final Yield Surface
p fyield = (0:pc);
% Effective Mean Pressure
q fyield = M*p fyield;
q yf = (MЋЖ2*(pc*p fyieldвИТp fyield.ЋЖ2)).ЋЖ0.5; % Deviatoric Stress
%% Normal Consolidation Line (NCL)
p NCL = (1:pc);
% Effective Mean Pressure
q NCL = p NCL*M;
% Deviatoric Stress
e NCL = (N вИТl*log(p NCL)) вИТ1;
% Void Ratio
%% Critical State Line (CSL)
p CSL = p NCL;
% Effective Mean Pressure
Gamma = 1+l*log(p(n))+void(n);
% Deviatoric Stress
e CSL = (Gamma вИТl*log(p CSL)) вИТ1;
% Void Ratio
%========================= Results and Plots =============================%
figure(1)
hold on
subplot(2,2,1)
plot(es,q,'k','LineWidth',2)
grid on
xlabel('Axial strain, $\varepsilon {11} $ [\%]','Interpreter','latex','fontsize',font)
ylabel('Deviatoric Stress, $q $ [kPa]','Interpreter','latex','fontsize',font)
subplot(2,2,3),
plot(p,q,'kвИТ','LineWidth',2);
grid on

De = De.*transverse

((MЋЖ2вИТ(3/2)*alpha d'*alpha d)*p);

Q = F;

%% Derivative of Plastic Potential

% Renaming

% Taken from Wheeler 2003

% Taken from Wheeler 2003

% Taken for Dafalias 2013
c7 = s;
% Taken for Dafalias 2013

% Remaning (Dafalias vs. Wheeler)

(c5*(dev Sig/p)вИТalpha d)*dQ dq);

c8*patm*p/peq*(alpha b 2вИТalpha d);
% Evolution Rule 3 вИТDafailis 2013
sig norm = norm(dev Sig/p);

n r = (dev Sig/p)/(norm(dev Sig/p));
alpha b 3 = M/c6*(1вИТexp(вИТc7*abs(eta)/M))*n r;
y3 =
c8*patm*p/peq*(alpha b 3вИТalpha d);

% Renaming
c7 = b;
% Renaming
c8 = R;
% Recovering of structure
c9 = Rtime;
% Renaming

% Defining boundary value of x

dx dt = heaviside(c8*x bвИТx)*(c8*x bвИТx)*c9*dt;% Recovering of structure

% Yield Surface by p0
dp0 dlam = (1+e0)/(lвИТk)*p0*dQ dp;
% p0 by Plastic Multiplier
dF dalpha = diffvec(alpha d, F);
% Yield Surface by Fabric
dF dx = diffvec(x, F);
% Yield Surface by Structure

function out = macaulay brackets(x)
out = x*heaviside(x);

function out = diffvec(Sig, Q)
for i = 1:1:length(Sig)
dfdv(i) = simplify(diff(Q,Sig(i)));
end
out = dfdv';

Changes to Implicit Integration
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%%% Modifications for utilizing implicit integration
%%%
%%% Assembling the residual and the Jacobi matrix
%%%
%%% By Jesper Bjerre вИТs102905
%%%
%%% Master Thesis
%%%
%%% DTU and NTNU
%%%
%%% Spring 2015
%%%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
% Vector containing unknowns
v = [Sig;Lambda;p0;alpha d;x];
% State parameters in previous and current stage
kappa = [p0;alpha d;x];
kappa0 = [p00; alpha d0; x0];
% Evolution in state parameters
dkappa dlamda = [dp0 dlamda; dalpha d dlamda; dx dlamda];
dkappa dt = [0;0;dx dt];
% Formulating the differential equaitions
r1 = Sig вИТSig0 вИТDe*de + De*diffvec(Q,Sig)*(lamвИТlam0);
r2 = kappaвИТkappa0 вИТdkappa dlamda*(lamвИТlam0) + dkappa dt*dt;
r3 = (lamвИТlam0) вИТdlamdt*dt; % For elastoвИТplastic: F
% Assembling the residual
r = [r1;r2;r3];
% Generate the FORTRAN code
fortran(r, 'file', 'Residuals.f');
matlabFunction(r, 'file', 'Residuals.m');
% Jacobian matrix вИТ15x15 matrix
J = jacobian(r,v);
fortran(J, 'file', 'Jacobian.f');

% Redefine previous SP
v(1:6) = вИТ1*Sig0(1:6)
% Add the new stresses
v0 = v
% Rename the time
dt = dTime
dn = 0
de(:) = 0
de(1:6) = вИТ1.0*Deps(1:6)
% Changing notation
I1 = v(1) + v(2) + v(3)
If (IsUndr.Eq.1) Then
Swp
= Swp0 + BulkW*(dEps(1) + dEps(2) + dEps(3))
Else
Swp = Swp0
End If
! Check if time increment or strain increment is too large
deabs = abs(de)
demax = max(deabs(1), deabs(2), deabs(3), deabs(4), deabs(5), deabs(6))
dtmax = dt
ne = nint(demax/1.0eвИТ4)
nt = nint(dtmax/1.0)
if (ne.eq.0) then
ne = 1
end if
if (nt.eq.0) then
nt = 1
end if
n = max(ne,nt)
de = de/n*1.0
dt = dt/n*1.0
do i = 1,n
call calcondition()
end do
if (checkv() == .true.) then
v = StVar0
else
Sig(1:6) = вИТ1.0*v(1:6)
end if
StVar = v
end if ! End IDTask 2
if (IDTask.eq.3) then ! Material stiffness matrix
v = StVar0
v(1:6) = вИТ1*Sig0(1:6)
call Dematrix(D)
end if ! End IDTask 3
if (IDTask.eq.4) then ! Return number of state variables
nStat = size(v);
end if ! End IDTask 4
if (IDTask.eq.5) then ! Inquire matrix properties
NonSym
= 0
% 1 for nonвИТsymmetric DвИТmatrix
iStrsDep = 1
% 1 for stress dependent DвИТmatrix

iTang
= 0
% 1 for tangent DвИТmatrix
iTimeDep = 1
% 1 for time dependent DвИТmatrix

if (IDTask.eq.6) then ! Dependent material stiffness matrix
v = StVar0
v(1:6) = вИТ1*Sig0(1:6)
call Dematrix(D)
end if ! End IDTask 6

subroutine Lode Angle()

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% subroutine Lode Angle LMN %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
subroutine Lode Angle LMN()
% Incorreborate the Lode Angle by LMN
use vars
use map
Implicit Double Precision (AвИТZ)
if (q == 0.0) then
M =
6.0*sin(phi*PI/180)/(3.0вИТsin(phi*PI/180))
else

x1 = 1.0/6.0*acos(вИТ1.0+27.0/2.0*(LMN beta**2.0*(1.0вИТLMN beta)**2.0)/

LMN = sqrt(3.0)/2.0*LMN beta/(sqrt(LMN beta**2.0вИТLMN beta+1.0))*1.0/
(cos(LMN theta))

(6*sin(phi*PI/180)/(3вИТsin(phi*PI/180)))*LMN

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% subroutine calcondition %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
subroutine calcondition()

function lvec(v, l) result(y)
integer i,l
double precision :: y
double precision, dimension(l) :: v
y = 0.0
do i=1,l
y = y + v(i)*v(i)
end do
end function

%вИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТ%
function heaviside(x) result(y) % Function from MATLAB
double precision x,y
if (x > 0.0) then
y = 1.0d0
else if (x == 0.0) then
y = 0.5d0
else
y = 0.0d0
end if
end function
%вИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТ%
% Ensure that sina(1.0) and sina*(вИТ1.0) gives a real number
function checkSINA(x) result(y)
double precision x,y
if (x > 1.0) then
y =
1.0d0
else if (x < вИТ1.0) then
y = вИТ1.0d0
else
y = x
end if
end function
%вИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТ%
function ifzero(x) result(y) ! Check for division with zero
double precision x,y
if (x == 0.0) then
y =
0.999999999999999
else
y = x
end if
end function

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%%% FORTRAN

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% Newton Raphson s z() %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
% NR to determine Dafalias 2013 evoluation rule of rotation (z and s)
subroutine Newton Raphson s z()
implicit none
real(kind=8), parameter :: tol = 1.dвИТ14
real(kind=8) :: res= 1.0
integer :: itere = 0
integer:: maxiter = 20
double precision xx, ff, dff, deltaxx
xx = 2.0
% Initial guess of xx
do while (res.gt.tol)
if(itere.gt.maxiter) stop
call fff(xx,ff)
call dfff (xx,dff)
deltaxx = ff/dff
% Compute Newton increment xx
xx = xx вИТdeltaxx
% Update xx
call fff(xx,ff)
res = abs(ff)
itere = itere + 1
end do
if (xx
== 2.0) then
xx = xx
else
s = xx
z = xx
end if
end subroutine
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% fff() %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
% Function I for Dafalias 2013 evoluation rule of rotation (z and s)
subroutine fff(xx,ff)
real(kind=8), intent(in) :: xx
real(kind=8), intent(out) :: ff
call mapping()
M1 = 6.0*sin(phi*PI/180)/(3вИТsin(phi*PI/180))
ff = aK0NC вИТM1/xx*(1вИТexp(вИТxx*abs(etaK0NC)/M1))
end subroutine
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% dfff() %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
% Function II for Dafalias 2013 evoluation rule of rotation (z and s)
subroutine dfff(xx,dff)
real(kind=8), intent(in) :: xx
real(kind=8), intent(out) :: dff
call mapping()
M1 = 6.0*sin(phi*PI/180)/(3вИТsin(phi*PI/180))
dff = M1*(1.0вИТexp(вИТxx*etak0nc/M1))/xx**2.0вИТetak0nc*exp(вИТxx*etak0nc/M1)/xx
end subroutine

F.3.
Unit Weight

F.3
Unit Weight
The unit weight has been determined through the following method:
ќ≥ = (1 + w)Swds
wds + Sw
ќ≥w
(F.1)
where ќ≥w is the unit weight of water, Sw is the degree of saturation, w is the water content and ds
is the relative density which may be determined by:
ds = ѕБs
ѕБw
(F.2)
where ѕБs is the grain density of the soil and ѕБw is the density of the water. Notice, Sr is modiпђБed
if the magnitude if higher than 100 %. This is done by adjusting the initial void ratio.

Transformation of rs to ris.

F.5
Transformation of rs to ris.
The intrinsic creep number is normally found by an incremental oedometer. However, no incre-
mental data is available and may be approximated through the CRS data. The intrinsic creep
number (ris) may be found through the creep number (rs). It may be assumed that the ratio
between the compression parameter and creep number is a constant:
ќї
rs
вЙИConstant
(F.3)
Hence, knowing the creep number (rs), and the compression parameters (ќї and ќїi) the intrinsic
creep number may be found through:
rsi = rs
mi
m
(F.4)
Notice that the secant modulus has been utilized and may be rewritten into the compression
modulus through Eq. (7.7). The secant modulus is determined as suggested by Janbu (1969).
Hence, it may be found through the inclination of the curves shown in Figure F.6-F.7.

Appendix G
Documentation of Calculations
This appendix contains additional predictions related to the embankment located at Ons√Єy which
have not been highlighted in the main report.
G.1
NGI Data
The performance of the SCA-R model has been compared to other soil models in Section 8. To
ensure a reliable comparison, the inпђВuence of the пђБnite element model itself should be investigated.
Hence, the utilized пђБnite element model should be controlled to see if similar predictions, as for the
пђБnite element model assembled by Mehli (2015b), may be achieved. The SSC model is an available
soil model in PLAXIS and may be used in such a veriпђБcation. Figure G.1 shows two simulations,
one performed by the пђБnite element model suggested by the Author and one by Mehli (2015b).

0.6
SSC
SSCNGI
Figure G.1: Comparison of assembled пђБnite element models through the SSC soil model. The graph shows
the vertical displacement just beneath the center of the embankment.
A small deviation near 0.1 m of vertical displacement may be observed on Figure G.1. This may
be a consequence of how the пђБll layers are activated. By investigating the path closely it looks as if
the two last пђБll layers are activated on the same time for the NGI model. However, the rest of the
curve is very similar. Hence, it may be concluded that the assembled пђБnite element model yields
a similar response as for the пђБnite element model suggested by Mehli (2015b).
